Similarly, Skanda (VI. 224. 3-51) closely follows Ä€Å›v. gr and YÄj.

Owing to the difference in details of Å›rÄddha in the several gá¹›hyasÅ«tras, the smá¹›tis and the purÄá¹‡as, an important question arises whether a person should perform a Å›rÄddha rite only in accordance with the gá¹›hyasÅ«tra of his own Veda or ÅšÄkhÄ or whether he may perform it after the inclusion (upasaá¹hÄra ) of the several items found in other sÅ«tras and smá¹›tis though not included in the kalpa or gá¹›hyasÅ«tra of his own ÅšÄkhÄ. This topic is discussed at great length by HemÄdri (on Å›rÄddha ) pp. 748-759 and briefly by MedhÄtithi on Manu II, 29 and XI.216, by the Mit, on YÄj. III. 325, AparÄrka p. 1053 and others. Those who are for strictly following the details in one’s own sÅ«tra alone argue as follows: If one were at liberty to include details other than those contained in one’s own sÅ«tra, the order of the details (krama) given in one’s sutra and the time specified for each detail would be interfered with. Besides, if the addition of details were permissible, one would be liable to the charge of giving up the usages of one’s own family handed down for generations. These objectors rely upon such passages as that of the Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara which states ‘He who violating (the dictates of ) his own sÅ«tra acts according to a sutra meant for others, treats thereby his own sage ( the author of his sÅ«tra ) as unauthoritative and incurs (the blame of) doing what is improper.’281 The smá¹›tis that contain additional details may be meant for those men who have no kalpa or gá¹›hyasÅ«tra of their own or they may be useful for ÅšÅ«dras.

Those who espouse the view that as far as possible all details found in several gá¹›hyasÅ«tras and smá¹›tis on one rite should be included by every one rely mainly on Jaimini II. 4. 8-33 which is called ‘Å›ÄkhÄntarÄdhikaraá¹‡anyÄya’ or ‘sarvaÅ›ÄkhÄpratyayanyÄya’. The principal sutra is Jai. II. 4. 9.282 It is established in this sÅ«tra that the prayojana (purpose ) or phala (reward to be secured) of the rite is the same in different sÅ«tras and smá¹›tis. For example, the rewards of Å›rÄddha are stated to be the same in all sÅ«tras and smrÌ¥tis (vide notes 787-790 above); then the dravya (materials) and the deity are the same (in PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha the deities are the paternal ancestors and the materials, viz. kuÅ›as, tilas, water, vessels, foods &c. are the same in all works).

The effort that is enjoined (the vidhi) is the same and the name (PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha, Ekoddiá¹£á¹­a Å›rÄddha &c.) is the same. Therefore, on account of these various signs one easily recognizes that the same rite is being dealt with by all sÅ«tras, even if many of them differ in details. It cannot be said that smá¹›tis will be useful for those who have no sutras of their own. Each man of the first three vará¹‡as is attached to some sÅ«tra or other by the tradition of his family or caste. Similarly, the smá¹›tis cannot be held to be meant for Å›Å«dras alone, since the smá¹›tis mainly deal with upanayana, the study of the Vedas, agnihotra and other matters with which a Å›Å«dra has no concern. Similarly, the objection that if details were allowed to be inserted from other sÅ«tras and smá¹›tis the krama and kÄla prescribed in one’s SÅ«tra might have to be set aside, the reply is contained in Jai. I. 3. 5-7 (explained at great length in H. of Dh. vol. III. pp. 841-848 )283. The Å›ruti says ‘after getting ready a bundle of kuÅ›a grass he prepares the vedi (altar)’. Here a certain order of acts is laid down. If after a bundle of kuÅ›as is got ready, the man has a sneeze, he has to sip water (perform Äcamana) immediately as laid down by Manu V. 145 and Vas. III. 38 before he prepares the vedi. So the objector says the krama will be interfered with. The reply is that the sequence (krama) is only a characteristic of the items in the Å›ruti passage, that all that is meant is that the making of the altar follows the getting ready of a kuÅ›a bundle, but that it is not meant that the making of the vedi should follow immediately after the bundle is got ready. Therefore, the conclusion is that whatever detail is not in conflict with one’s own Å›ÄkhÄ or sutra may be included in the rite one is about to perform, but if there is a direct conflict, then one should stick to one’s own sÅ«tra. As observed by KÄtyÄyana ‘whatever is declared, whether in great detail or in brief, in one’s own gá¹›hyasÅ«tra about a rite, if he performs that much, it may be taken that he has done all that is required of him; but what is not handed down in one’s Å›ÄkhÄ (or sÅ«tra) but is contained in another Å›ÄkhÄ and is not in conflict with one’s own Å›ÄkhÄ, should be performed by the learned, as in the case of agnihotra and the like’284.

The first verse of Katyayana may be explained as applying where one’s Å›ÄkhÄ or sÅ«tra deals with a matter without requiring any addition or as the next best course where one is not able to supplement one’s sÅ«tra owing to some unseen cause or owing to human difficulties. The Saá¹…graha says that where different items of the Å›rÄddha rite are declared to be performed at different times and there is no agreement among the sages, one should follow one’s gá¹›hyasutra, but where one’s gá¹›hyasÅ«tra is silent as to a certain item or as to the stage when it is to be performed, there is an option and one may follow the opinion of some gage as to that item285. It appears that even the ancient gá¹›hya sÅ«tras exhibit tendencies that led to the sarvaÅ›ÄkhÄ-pratyaya-nyÄya e.g. AÅ›v. gr. (q. in n. 966) employs several passages (such as ‘Tilosi’, ‘YÄdivyÄ,’ which do not occur in the á¹šgveda.)

It is owing to this maxim of ‘sarvaÅ›ÄkhÄ-pratyayanyÄya’ that medieval digests go on heaping up details from all smá¹›tis and purÄá¹‡as in their descriptions of the several Å›rÄddhas, the procedures of which were not originally very extensive.

The KÅ«rmapurÄá¹‡a286 provides that the performer should before noontime is past bring to his house the invited brÄhmaá¹‡as who have shaved themselves and pared their nails, should offer them materials for brushing the teeth and request them to sit down on separate seats, then give them oil and water for bathing. It will be seen that here the purÄá¹‡a has added several details that were not provided for by the Ä€Å›v. grÌ¥. (n. 966), Manu (III. 208 ), YÄj. (I. 226) and even by some of the purÄá¹‡as such as the VarÄha 14, 8, which begin the procedure with seating the brÄhmaá¹‡as in the afternoon on seats after welcoming them. Many more examples could be given, but that attempt is not made owing to considerations of space.

Before proceeding to give some idea as to how the pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha was dealt with in medieval and modern times, observations must be made on several relevant matters, on which there is a difference of views or which are important in a general way.

When the invited brÄhmaá¹‡as come afternoon, it is provided in some of the purÄá¹‡as that two maá¹‡á¸alas should be made in front of the performer’s residence for receiving the brÄhmaá¹‡as. For example, the NÄradapurÄá¹‡a states287 the maá¹‡á¸ala for a brÄhmaá¹‡a performer should be square (four cornered) in size, for a ká¹£atriya triangular, circular for a vaiÅ›yÃ  and for a Å›Å«dra mere sprinkling of the ground with water is enough.’ The maá¹‡á¸alas should be made on ground smeared with cowdung and with water mixed with cow’s urine. Of the two maá¹‡á¸alas one should be on the northern side sloping towards the north and the other on the southern side sloping towards the south. On the northern maá¹‡á¸ala kuÅ›as with points towards the east should be placed along with aká¹£ata (unbroken or whole) grains and on southern one double-folded kuÅ›as should be placed along with sesamum grains. The northern maá¹‡á¸ala should generally be two cubits on each side while the southern one should be four cubits on each side. The brÄhmaá¹‡a or brÄhmaá¹‡as that represent the ViÅ›vedevÄh should be honoured in the northern maá¹‡á¸ala first by the performer himself with water for washing their feet after bending his right knee and the brÄhmaá¹‡as representing the pitá¹›s should be then honoured in the southern maá¹‡á¸ala with water for washing the feet (hence called pÄdya) after bending his left knee.

‘SaptÄrcis’ mantra

A certain order of words about referring to the ancestors in offering a seat, offering kuÅ›as on seat, and arghya is laid down by Bá¹›haspati292, some of the purÄá¹‡as and the digests. An important question which exercised the minds of many medieval writers was ‘who was the real recipient (sampradÄna)294 of the offerings made in Å›rÄddha, whether the brÄhmaá¹‡as or the pitá¹›s’. Relying on the words of the Ä€Å›v. gr. Vide Sm. C. (Å›rÄddha pp. 447-449 ), Åšr. Pr. pp. 30-31 for a discussion of these two view points. It should, however, be noted that the water given to the brÄhmaá¹‡as and the daká¹£iá¹‡Ä given to them were only meant for the brÄhmaá¹‡as, the former for purification and the latter for the inexhaustible merit that daká¹£iá¹‡Ä to brÄhmaá¹‡as conferred.

It is to be noted that in the invocation (ÄvÄhana) of the pitá¹›s there is a divergence of views as to the mantras to be employed and also as to the stage when it is to be made. HemÄdri (Å›rÄddha, pp. 1254-56) states that there were five different views on the latter point, the three most important of which were that ÄvÄhana should come before giving darbhas as Äsana on the left side of the seats of the pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡as or after giving such darbhas or after agnaukaraá¹‡a. As regards the former (viz. the mantras), YÄj. (I. 232-233), the BrahmÄá¹‡á¸apurÄá¹‡a and several others say that the invocation mantra is ‘uÅ›antas-tvÄ’ (á¹šg. X. 16. 12, VÄj. S. 19. 70, Tai. S. The Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasutra296 (73. 10-12) states “the performer after having received permission from the brÄhmaá¹‡as should invite the Manes. Mantras of ÄvÄhana

HemÄdri (Å›rÄddha pp. 1260-1267) points out how the mantras differ according to different writers.

There is a great deal of discussion about agnaukaraá¹‡a mentioned in Yaj. I. 236-237. The Mit. points out that if a man has kept Å›rauta fires by the sarvÄdhÄna method then in the pÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha that he offers after piá¹‡á¸apitá¹›yajÃ±a he offers homa in the Daká¹£iá¹‡Ägni, as he has no aupÄsana (i.e. gá¹›hya) fire and supports this by quoting a passage which occurs in the Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara-purÄá¹‡a297. Vide HemÄdri (Å›rÄddha pp. 1328-1344), BÄlambhatti (on ÄcÄra p. 518). The TodarÄnanda (Å›rÄddhasaukhya ) following Manu III. 282 states that one who is an Agnihotrin cannot perform a pÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha on any day except on darÅ›a (i.e, on amÄvÄsyÄ).

Another important question on which there is great divergence concerns the number of Ähutis to be offered in agnaukaraá¹‡a, the deities to whom the homa was to be offered, the order in which the deities were to be named and the form of words to be used. On these matters there appears to have been divergent views from very ancient times. According to the Åšat. Br. It is therefore that a verse of KÄtyÄyana301 says “whether to employ the word ‘svÄhÄ’ or the words ‘svadhÄ namaá¸¥’, whether and when to wear the sacred chord in the yajÃ±opavÄ«ta form or prÄcinÄvita form and what was to be the number of Ähutis-all these have to be understood from one’s own sÅ«tra.” The difference of views which is noticed as far back as the BrÄhmanas persists in the sÅ«tras, smá¹›tis, purÄá¹‡as and is briefly indicated here.

Number of Ähutis

The Ap. Gr. S. (quoted above in n. 981 ) speaks of thirteen Ähutis, seven with food and six with clarified butter. Two Ähutis only are mentioned by AÅ›v. Åšrauta II. 6. 12 (quoted in note 974), Asv. gx. IV. 7.20, Åšaá¹‡khaLikhita302 Kathaka-gá¹›hya, NÄrada-purÄá¹‡a (pÄrvÄrdha 28. 48 ) and MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya-purÄá¹‡a. Three Ähutis are mentioned by most of the smá¹›tis and purÄá¹‡as, such as Baud. Dh. S. II. 14.7, 1031 San. Åšrauta IV. 3 and gá¹›hya IV. 1. 13, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh, S. 73. 12, Manu III. 211,VarahapurÄá¹‡a303 (14.21-22), BrahmÄá¹‡á¸a-purÄá¹‡a (UpodghatapÄda 11.93-94), Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara I. 140.19. It is here that various combinations of the order of the deities and of the words ‘svÄhÄ’ and ‘svadhÄ’ occur. The texts of a few works are cited below for illustration. Some give the order as ‘Soma accompanied by the pitá¹›s, Agni the carrier of kavya, Yama Angiras;’ while others give it as ‘Agni the carrier of kavya, Soma accompanied by the pitá¹›s, Yama Vaivasvata &c.’ It is further provided that the Ähuti to Agni is made to the southern side of the fire, to Soma on the northern part of it and in the middle of the two sides to Vaivasvata (Yama )304.

Various rules of etiquette about serving food, about the way brÄhmaá¹‡as are to eat and related matters have been laid down from ancient times and are observed even now. The Sm. C. (p. 465-470 ), Hemadri (ÅšrÄddha pp. 1367-1384), the Sr. Pr. pp. 116-123 and other digests contain elaborate rules on these matters. YÄj. I. 237 prescribes that what remains after offering homa should be served in the plates meant for the brÄhmaá¹‡as representing the Fathers and the plates should preferably be of silver.

KÄtyÄyana says that the performer who has no fire (either Å›rauta or smÄrta ) should offer the homa on the hand of the most eminent among the pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡as to the accompaniment of a mantra and should offer the remainder in the plates of the other pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡as305. The Sm. C. notes that Yama and the VÄyupurÄá¹‡a say that the homa should be in the hand of the daiva brÄhmaá¹‡a and that on account of this conflict there is an option. It is further provided that a portion of the food from which agnaukaraá¹‡a was effected should be kept aside for making piá¹‡á¸as (MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya and Garuda). The food should be served by the performer himself wearing the sacred thread in the yajÃ±opavÄ«ta form or by his wife of the same caste or by some attendant who is purified. Manu (III. 224-229, 231-233, 236-238) contains several rules306. ‘Himself holding the vessels full of food with both hands the performer should slowly place them near the brÄhmaá¹‡as seated for dinner while thinking of his ancestors. That food, which is brought near the brÄhmaá¹‡as without (the containing vessel being held in ) both hands, is snatched away by wicked asuras. The performer being himself pure and concentrating his mind (only on the serving of food ) should place the vessels containing the side dishes viz. broths, vegetables, milk, curds, ghee and honey on the ground and not on a wooden stool or the like). The vessels placed on the ground should contain various kinds of food (such as sweetmeats, pÄyasa), fruits and roots, delicious flesh, spiced or perfumed drinks.

Rules about serving food

Having brought near the brÄhmaá¹‡as all the vessels containing food, he should serve it in the plates declaring the characteristic of each kind of food (saying ’this is sweet, this is sour’ &c.). The performer while serving should not shed tears (remembering his ancestors ), he should not become angry, should not utter falsehood, he should not touch the vessel containing ) food with his foot and should not serve food with a violent jerk. Whatever is liked by the brÄhmaá¹‡as should be served to them without any grumbling and he should talk about Brahma (or about Vedic riddles ), because that is agreeable to the pitá¹›s. The performer himself, being pleased, should ( by his sweet speech ) gladden the brÄhmaá¹‡as, should make them partake of food without hurry, and should urge them on frequently (to take more food) by declaring the good points of the various items of food. All food should be steaming hot and the brÄhmaá¹‡as should partake of it silently and should not themselves speak of the good points of the food even when questioned by the performer. When the food is hot, when the brÄhmaá¹‡as eat in silence and when the good qualities of the food are not declared by the brÄhmaá¹‡as, the Fathers partake of it. When a brÄhmaá¹‡a (at a Å›rÄddha dinner) partakes of food, having his head covered with a garment (a turban &c)or facing the south or having his sandals on, evil spirits partake of the food (and not the Fathers).’ As early as Gaut., it is provided that the food for the brÄhmaá¹‡as should be of the highest sort and should be enriched by condiments and flavoured.

Some of the other rules are : The PrajÄpati-smá¹›ti (verses 57-62 ) states who should cook the food for the Å›rÄddha dinner. The wife, any fortunate (or handsome) woman of the performer’s gotra whose husband is living and who has a son or brother and is devoted to waiting upon her elders, the performer’s teacher’s wife, his maternal uncle’s wife, paternal or maternal aunt, his sister, his daughter or daughter-in-law, all of them having their husbands alive, should engage in the cooking, of the food for Å›rÄddha. Women of good family, who have a large progeny, whose husbands are alive and who are about 50 years old or these women if they have become widows, and the wife of one’s paternal uncle or brother, one’s mothers (natural or step-mothers ) and the mothers of one’s father may cook the Å›rÄddha food and also a woman of the same gotra who is mild by temper. In the AnuÅ›Äsana parva (92.15) it is stated that a woman belonging to a gotra other than that of the deceased should not be employed for cooking Å›rÄddha food. One’s brother, paternal uncle, brother’s son, sister’s son, one’s son, pupil, one’s daughter’s son or daughter’s husband may also engage in cooking the Å›rÄddha food, but not a woman who wears a white and wet garment, who has let her hair loose, who does not wear a bodice, who is ill or who has bathed her head. Before the brÄhmaá¹‡as begin to eat, the food should be first served in the plates for the VaiÅ›vadeva brÄhmaá¹‡as and then in those of the pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡as (Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 173. 13-14), but once the brÄhmaá¹‡as have begun to eat there is no such precedence; whatever each brÄhmaá¹‡a may be noted as requiring should be served to him (as indicated by Manu III, 231). The performer of the Å›rÄddha when serving food to the brÄhmaá¹‡as (including pitá¹›ya) wears his sacred thread in the upavita form. Though it is said that the food should be hot, that does not apply to such eatables as curds, fruits and roots and flavoured307 drinks (as Åšaá¹‡kha XIV.13 and SkandapurÄá¹‡a say ). As HemÄdri (Å›rÄddha p. 1371) points out, the serving of food in a Å›rÄddha rite should be done with the right hand supported by the left; besides, nothing should be served with the bare hand or with a single hand, but with a wooden ladle or other utensil (but not an iron one). All cooked food, all side dishes and ghee should be served with a ladle (and not with the bare hand) but not water or eatables like laddus. No salt was to be directly served (Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S, 79.12). In KÄtyÄyana’s ÅšrÄddha-sÅ«tra it is provided[^1036] “The food that remains after agnaukaraá¹‡a should be served in the plates of the pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡as and the performer should touch each plate for those brÄhmaá¹‡as after repeating the mantra ’the earth is thy vessel, the sky is the cover, I offer nectar in the brÄhmaá¹‡a’s nectar-like mouth; svÄhÄ’. Then the performer plunges the (right) thumb of the pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡a in the food (the remains of the homa in fire) with the á¹›k or yajus addressed to Viá¹£á¹‡u; and after scattering sesamum grains all round the place where the dinner is to go on with the mantra (the asuras and rÄká¹£asas) are struck and driven away’ he (the performer) should serve hot food very much liked (by the departed or by the brÄhmaá¹‡as)”.

Åšaá¹‡kha-Likhita provide310 that brÄhmaá¹‡as (eating Å›rÄddha food ) should not declare the good or bad points of the food served, should not say what is untrue, should not praise each other, nor should they say ’there is plenty of food and drink (and so do not serve more food ), except by signs made with the hand.’ Whatever is offered in a brÄhmaá¹‡a’s hand (by way of agnaukaraá¹‡a) and whatever food is placed in the plates should be mixed up and eaten. The MatsyapurÄá¹‡a and some other works provide that the brÄhmaá¹‡as should pronounce blessings on the performer (who faces the east) after they have sipped water and have been given water, flowers and aká¹£ata grains. The performer prays ‘May our Fathers be not terrible (i, e, be kind) to us. The brÄhmaá¹‡as reply “let it be so’. The performer prays ‘May our family grow’, ‘May donors increase in our family and also food’, â€˜May these blessings turn out to be true’. To all these the brÄhmaá¹‡as reply after each one of the prayers311 ‘May it be so’. There are rules about the time when the leavings or particles of food in the plates of the brÄhmaá¹‡as that ate the dinner should be removed or swept away. Vas. Rules about removal of leavings

Hemadri (ÅšrÄddha, p. 1512 )312 therefore provides that if the performer has another house the leavings may not be removed till sunset, but if he has only one house, they may be removed after the brÄhmaá¹‡as have gone away (vide YÄj. There were several views as to the exact stage at which the offerings of piá¹‡á¸as were to be made. According to SÄn. Gr. IV. 1.9., Ä€Å›v. gr. IV. 8. 12, 1047 Åšaá¹‡kha 14.11, Manu III. 260-261, YÄj. There is another conflict of views as regards the ancestors to whom piá¹‡á¸as are to be offered at the Å›rÄddha on amÄvÄsya. Most of the ancient Vedic works refer only to the three paternal ancestors as the devatÄs of the PÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha and not to any other relatives. The answer to both these questions so far as the Vedas and BrÄhmanas are concerned is in the negative. Vide Tai. S. I. 8. 5.1, Tai. Br. I. 3. 10, II, 6. 16, VÄj. S. 19. 36-37, Sat. Br. Ancestors to whom piá¹‡á¸as were offered

The question when the wives of ancestors became associated with the male ancestors cannot be solved satisfactorily. The pitÄmahi is not mentioned in the extant Vedic Literature. But it is certain that the wives of male ancestors had come to be associated with their husbands in the sÅ«tra period. For example, the Hir. Gr. Participation of females in piá¹‡á¸as offered

If the offering of piá¹‡á¸as to women ancestors were borrowed from the Anvaá¹£á¹­akya this item would have figured in all sÅ«tras just as Aá¹£á¹­akÄÅ›raddha does. Some of the smá¹›tis emphasize the participation of the wives of paternal ancestors in the pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha offerings. As regards the mantra to be recited when offering each of the piá¹‡á¸as there is some divergence of views, which reaches far into antiquity. The piá¹‡á¸a335 is offered to each ancestor after stating his gotra, his relationship to the performer and name (all in the vocative). According to several writers the form of the presentation of the piá¹‡á¸a is ’this is for thee, O Father! N.N. (by name) and so and so by gotra.’ In the Tai. S. I. 8.5 1, in the Ap. M. P. In the Gobhila-gá¹›hya IV. 3.6 and KhÄdiragá¹›hya the formula is still longer ‘This piá¹‡á¸a is thine, O father! and of those who follow thee and of those whom thou followest. 337 To thee, Svadha!’. Mantras at offering piá¹‡á¸as

It has been already noted above (n. 781) how the Sat. Certain matters pertaining to piá¹‡á¸as must be stated in one place once for all. There is a good deal of discussion about the size of the piá¹‡á¸as. About the final disposal (pratipatti) of the piá¹‡á¸as several views were entertained. We have already seen (note 780 ) that the VÄj. On the question as to what is the principal item in the Å›rÄddha there are three views. Some, like Govindaraja350, hold that feeding of brÄhmaá¹‡as is the principal thing and they rely on Manu III. 129 which says ‘One may feed even a single learned brÄhmaá¹‡a in a rite for gods and manes and not many who do not know vedic mantras; by doing so he reaps abundant reward’. Here the reward or fruit is associated with feeding a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a. There is a maxim351 of the PÅ«rvamimÄá¹sÄ that whatever is mentioned in proximity with an action that has a reward or fruit but has no special reward of its own, is subsidiary (anga) to what is declared to have a reward.’ Besides, there is no piá¹‡á¸adÄna in certain Å›rÄddhas such as ÄmaÅ›rÄddha, Å›rÄddha on the YugÄdi days352.

Others like Karka hold the view that piá¹‡á¸adÄna is the principal item in Å›rÄddha. They rely upon the fact that in the Å›rÄddha at GayÄ it is the offering of piá¹‡á¸as that is the most important matter and upon the fact that in the Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra (78. 52-53, and 85. 65-66) and in several PurÄá¹‡as such as the VarÄha (13.50), Viá¹£á¹‡u (III. 14.22-23), Brahma (220.31-32), Viá¹£á¹‡udharmottara (I. 145.3-4) the pitá¹›s are stated to have expressed a deep yearning for a son who would offer them piá¹‡á¸as at GayÄ and water on sacred rivers &c. (vide note 911). Further support is sought for this view in the fact that in the Å›rÄddha on the birth of a son and in Å›rÄddha offered even by a good ÅšÅ«dra the feeding of brÄhmaá¹‡as is forbidden, A third view is that both the feeding of brÄhmaá¹‡as and the offering of piá¹‡á¸as are the principal matters in a Å›rÄddha. The Gobhila-smá¹›ti, after stating353 the other two views and some of the reasons therefor, tells us that the author of it is himself in favour of this third view. In cases where the word Å›rÄddha is employed and there is neither brÄhmaá¹‡a feeding nor piá¹‡á¸adÄna as in Daiva Å›rÄddha, the word Å›rÄddha is applied in a secondary sense (gauá¹‡a). Vide HemÄdri (Å›raddha) pp. 157-160. In the Dharmapradipa it is said that among the followers of the Yajurveda (i.e. VÄjasaneyins) the offering of piá¹‡á¸as is principal, among the followers of the á¹šgveda the feeding of brÄhmaá¹‡as is principal, while among the followers of the SÄmaveda both are principal. In this way Å›rÄddha partakes of two characters viz. it is a yÄga (sacrifice) and also354 a gift (dÄna). Haradatta, HemÄdri, Kapardi and others appear to hold that all three (bhojaá¹‡a piá¹‡á¸adÄna and agnaukaraá¹‡a) are principal. Vide SamskÄraratnamÄlÄ p. 1003.

The MatsyapurÄá¹‡a says in a famous passage ‘ancestors from the fourth (i.e. the father, grand-father and great-grand father of the great-grandfather of performer ) are entitled only to the wipings of the articles of food (sticking to the hand of the performer of Å›rÄddha);

Persons that are lepabhÄjaá¸¥

the father, (the grand-father and great-grandfather) are entitled to the piá¹‡á¸a; the offerer of the piá¹‡á¸a is the 7th; thus sapiá¹‡á¸a relationship extends to seven generations355. The MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya-purÄá¹‡a states that three ancestors beginning from the paternal grandfather of the paternal grandfather enjoy the wipings (of the hand of the performer when he offers piá¹‡á¸as)356. The BrahmapurÄá¹‡a (220. 84-86) has similar verses. A special rule was provided by Devala quoted in ÅšrÄddhakalpalatÄ (p. 14) that if the father or mother became a mleccha (by forcible or voluntary conversion &c.) one should not observe any impurity for him or her and no Å›rÄddha should be offered and when offering the three piá¹‡á¸as in place of the father the name of Viá¹£á¹‡u should be uttered.

One question that exercised the minds of well-known writers was whether the daily VaiÅ›vadeva should be performed before starting the Å›rÄddha ceremonies or after the latter were finished. We have to remember first that several works state that rites for pitá¹›s deserve pre-eminence over those for the gods358. Manu (III. 265 ) states359 that after the brÄhmaá¹‡as have been dismissed the performer of Å›rÄddha shall offer the (daily) domestic bali offering and that this is the settled rule of dharma. MedhÄtithi explains that the word ‘bali’ is merely illustrative. The MatsyapurÄá¹‡a360 17.61, the VarÄhapurÄá¹‡a (14.43), Skanda VII. 1. 266. 101-102, Devala, KÄrá¹£á¹‡Äjini and others provide that after the rites for the fathers are finished Vaisvadeva should be performed. When VaiÅ›vadeva is performed after the completion of Å›rÄddha rites, it is to be offered from the food that remains after being utilized for the Å›rÄddha dinner. But HemÄdri (pp. 1058-1065) holds a long discussion and arrives at the following conclusions. In the case of an ÄhitÄgni (p. 1064), VaiÅ›vadeva is to be performed before Å›rÄddha except in the case of the Å›rÄddha on the 11th day after death. In the case of one who has not kept the sacred fires there are three optional times for VaiÅ›vadeva, viz. after agnau karaá¹‡a or after the vikira (scattering of food on darbhas for those dying without the saá¹skÄras being performed for them), or after the brÄhmaá¹‡as are dismissed on the completion of the Å›rÄddha (p. 1064). If VaiÅ›vadeva is to be performed before Å›rÄddha or in the midst of it, then the cooking of food must be done separately for VaiÅ›vadeva and for Å›rÄddha. In the case of all, whether sÄgnika or anagnika, VaiÅ›vadeva must be performed with the remainder of the food left after use in Å›rÄddha, if the former is performed after the latter. There were sages like PaithÄ«nasi361 that held that VaiÅ›vadeva should not be performed with the food cooked for Å›rÄddha without first offering the food to the brÄhmaá¹‡as invited for srÄdda dinner i.e. VaiÅ›vadeva must be performed after Å›rÄddha if the same food is to be used for the former.

The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (III. p. 459 ) states that as most smá¹›tis place the performance of VaiÅ›vadeva after Å›rÄddha and as many writers of commentaries and digests such as MedhÄtithi and Smá¹›tiratnÄvali do the same all should perform VaiÅ›vadeva after finishing Å›rÄddha.

Modern PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha

It is now necessary to illustrate how pÄrvaá¹‡a Å›rÄddha is performed in modern times. Here the greatest variety prevails. The details of the rite vary in the different parts of India. They vary also according as the performer belongs to a particular Veda or a particular recension of the Veda, they vary according to the particular eminent writer that is followed and further additions are made according as the performer is a Vaiá¹£á¹‡ava or a Åšaiva. Such differences have to be passed over here, particularly because they are not material. We saw above that the PÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha was very simple in the days of the BrÄhmaá¹‡as and most of the sÅ«tras. There was then no express mention of the worship of ViÅ›vedevÄá¸¥ in PÄrvaá¹‡a Å›rÄddha, or of the maternal ancestors or of the wives of any ancestors. Gradually these came to be added and by the time of the YÄjÃ±avalkyasmá¹›ti there was special invocation of the ViÅ›vedevÄá¸¥. But these latter were probably not expressly divided into groups at the time of that work. During the smá¹›ti period groups of ViÅ›vedevÄá¸¥ came to be assigned to different kinds of Å›rÄddhas (vide p. 457 note 1018). The PurÄá¹‡as prescribed several PaurÄnic mantras for being recited during the Å›rÄddha rite, such as the verse ‘Ägacchantu’ (note 984) and the verse ‘devatÄbhyaá¸¥ pitá¹›bhyaÅ›ca’ (p. 458 note 1020). Then there arose the principle of the PÅ«rvamiá¹ƒÄmsÄ that all rites described in the different Å›ÄkhÄs and sÅ«tras constitute but one rite, and that one may take over from another Å›ÄkhÄ or sÅ«tra details described therein, which are not opposed to one’s own Å›ÄkhÄ or sutra (vide pp. 453-55 notes 1011-1013). This principle tended to make the Å›rÄddha rites all inclusive and the whole procedure became swollen to an enormous extent. How even the slightest variation causes difference in the details may be illustrated by an example. In MithilÄ villages even among poor men eleven brÄhmaá¹‡as are invited for pÄrvapa-Å›rÄddha, but it is often difficult to secure a very learned man (who is called pÄtra or mahÄpÄtra) to officiate. In such a case where a mahÄpÄtra or a pÄtra brÄhmaá¹‡a is not available the Å›rÄddha be called apÄtraka-pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha (for which really there is no Å›astric authority) and is distinguished from the sapÄtraka pÄrvaá¹‡a Å›rÄddha in several matters, two of which in the case of the VÄjasaneyins may be stated here. Though the ÅšrÄddhasÅ«tra of KÄtyÄyana, which is of special authority among VÄjasaneyins, declares (at the end of kaá¹‡á¸ikÄ III) that the brÄhmaá¹‡as are to be dismissed at the end of the Å›rÄddha with the verse ‘VÄje vÄje’ (VÄj. In Rudradhara’s ÅšrÄddhaviveka (pp. 138-146) the ‘apÄtraka pÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha-prayoga’ is set out at length.

Comparing the above rather elaborate and exhaustive procedure with the procedure of pratisÄmvatsarikaÅ›rÄddha as practised in Madras in these days only a few points of differences in detail emerge. In the Madras procedure the numerous mantras as set out above (p. 448) do not occur nor does the Tai. Ä€ passage occur therein, but the verses in praise of the dust of brÄhmaá¹‡a’s feet do occur. Most of the mantras both Vedic and PaurÄá¹‡ic are the same. The blessings (ÄÅ›irvÄdas) in the Madras manual are more elaborate and include even the performer’s cattle in the blessing of health and long life. In the Madras manual several mantras are set out as ‘annasÅ«kta’ mantras in the procedure itself. The Madras paddhati adds at the end the well known verse ‘kÄyena vÄcÄ manasendriyairvÄ’.

The procedure for Yajurvedins of the MÄdhyandina ÅšÄkhÄ in Bengal as set out by Raghunandana in his Yajurvedi-Å›rÄddhatattva is entirely based on the 2nd and 3rd kaá¹‡á¸ikÄs of the ÅšrÄddha-sÅ«tra of KatyÄyana. The PÄryaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha-prayoga in HalÄyudha’s BrÄhmaá¹‡asarvasva closely follows KÄtyÄyana and I am assured by a careful student of DharmaÅ›Ästra that the procedure of pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha followed by Madhyandinas at Bhatpara in Western Bengal is in strict conformity with the procedure laid down by Raghunandana. The ÅšrÄddhasÅ«tra of KÄtyÄyana is translated here with some explanations and additions made by HalÄyudha and Raghunandana and the whole text, being not very extensive, is given in the note below398.

The ÅšrÄddhasÅ«tra of KÄtyÄyana

“The pÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha is preceded by the rite for ViÅ›vedevas. The procedure follows that of Piá¹‡á¸apitá¹›yajÃ±a399. In the rites for the pitá¹›s double-folded darbhas are to be used (while in VaiÅ›vadevika rite straight darbhas are used). Whenever any thing is to be gifted, the performer (in all daiva as well as pitá¹›ya rites ) wears a pavitra and makes the gift sitting. (When questions are to be asked the performer puts the question to the most eminent in the row of diners (to the best daiva brÄhmaá¹‡a in daiva rites and to the best pitá¹›ya brÄhmaá¹‡a in pitá¹›ya rites) or he may put the question to all (and the reply is given by one or by all). PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha in Bengal

It is to be noted that in the Bengal Manual of PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha based on the BrÄhmaá¹‡asarvasva of HalÄyudha and also on Raghunandana’s Yajurvedi-Å›rÄddhatattva the following details are added after the piá¹‡á¸as are placed on the darbhas and before the dismissal of the brÄhmaá¹‡as. He turns towards the north and says ‘O pitá¹›s, regale yourselves here and come like bulls each to his portion.’ Then he turns round to his former position and says ’the pitá¹›s regaled themselves and came like bulls each to his own share.’ Then he loosens411 the side of his dhoti which has been tucked up and then folds up his hands (i.e. offers six namaskÄras) with the mantra412 ‘Namo vaá¸¥ pitaro rasÄya’ (VÄj. S. I. 32). He smells413 the piá¹‡á¸as and gives the middle one to his wife, if she desires a son, with the mantra ‘Ädhatta’ (Vaj. S. II. 33).

From considerations of space the procedure of pÄrvaá¹‡a Å›rÄddha among Hiraá¹‡yakeÅ›ins in modern times is passed over here. Moreover, it closely resembles the procedure in Ä€Å›v. Gr. set out above, the principal difference being that some of the mantras are different. The Saá¹skÄraratnamÄlÄ of GopinÄtha deals with this from p. 985 onwards. But one noticeable feature of this last work is that of making hair-splitting distinctions. On p. 985 it refers to two Å›rÄddhas (other than piá¹‡á¸a-pitá¹›yajÃ±a) to be performed on amÄvÄsyÄ, viz. mÄsi-Å›rÄddha and mÄsika-Å›rÄddha, the first being described by the Dharmasutra of Hiraá¹‡yakesin and the second by the GrÌ¥hyasÅ«tra. GopinÄtha further says that mÄsikaÅ›raddha is the model of the other Å›rÄddhas described in the Gá¹›hyasÅ«tra and mÄsiÅ›rÄddha is the model of Å›rÄddhas described in the DharmaÅ›Ästras, such as MahÄlayaÅ›rÄddha or SÄá¹vatsarika-Å›rÄddha, that DarÅ›aÅ›rÄddha itself is mÄsi-Å›rÄddha (p. 988) and that mÄsika-Å›rÄddha may be performed on each darÅ›a or on any one darÅ›a day in a year and that mÄsi-Å›rÄddha follows immediately after Piá¹‡á¸apitá¹›yajÃ±a as laid down by Manu (III. 122) and mÄsikaÅ›rÄddha may be performed after mÄsi-Å›rÄddha.

In modern times no one performs mÄsiÅ›rÄddha or mÄsika-Å›rÄddha strictly in accordance with the ancient rules. ÅšrÄddha is supposed to be performed by feeding a brÄhmaá¹‡a and giving him a daká¹£iá¹‡Ä of a few annas. The ÅšrÄddhatattva (Jiv., part 1 p. 254) provides, after quoting Matsya and Bhaviá¹£ya, that, if a person is unable to perform a pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha every month, he should perform one at least thrice a year when the sun is in the zodiacal signs of KanyÄ, Kumbha and VrÌ¥á¹£abha and that if he is unable to perform even thrice then he should perform at least once when the sun is in the sign of KanyÄ.

The DayabhÄga theory of spiritual benefit

The two meanings of sapiá¹‡á¸a given by the Mit, and the DÄyabhÄga have already been explained at great length in H. of Dh. vol. II, pp. 452-458 and pp. 472-477. The DÄyabhÄga propounded the theory that whoever conferred greater spiritual benefit on the deceased by the performance of Å›rÄddhas and the offering of piá¹‡á¸as was entitled to be preferred as an heir to the deceased’s wealth. The Mit. said that heirship depended on blood relationship and the nearest in blood to the deceased was the preferential heir. But even under the Mit. whoever took the wealth of the deceased was bound to pay his debts (Yaj. II, 51 ) and to offer Å›rÄddha and piá¹‡á¸a to him. This subject and the several propositions deduced from the texts have been dealt with in H. of Dh. vol. III, pp. 734-745.

One thing to be remembered is that in determining the preferential right regard is to be had to the capacity of a person and the efficacy of the piá¹‡á¸as when offered. After a person takes the wealth of the deceased, there is no legal machinery to enforce his duty to offer piá¹‡á¸as414. It was argued in Nalinaksha v. Rajani Kanto 35 C. W. N. 726 that, if an heir does not offer piá¹‡á¸as or refuses to offer them to his ancestor. he was not entitled under Hindu Law to succeed to him. This argument was repelled by the judges with the remark that there was no authority for such a proposition and that the right to inheritance is based under the DÄyabhÄga not on offering piá¹‡á¸a but on the capacity to offer it (p. 729). Vide Gooroo Gobinda Saha v. Anand Lal 5 Beng. L. R. 15 (F. B.) and Digamber v. Motilal 9 Cal. 563 (F. B.) for the statement that the principle of spiritual benefit is the sole foundation of the theory of inheritance propounded in the DÄyabhÄga.

But in certain later cases such as Akshayachandra v. Hari Das 35 Cal, 721 it was rightly pointed out that spiritual benefit is not always the guiding principle under the Bengal school of law, that the principle of spiritual efficacy as the guiding principle fails in all classes of female relations such as the wife, the daughter and the mother whose rights are really based on special texts and that the reason for inheritance by a re-united coparcener was not spiritual benefit but was based on a quasi contract and affection.

The question about how Å›rÄddha was to be performed if one or more of the three paternal ancestors were alive engaged the thoughts of writers from very ancient times. The Ä€Å›v.415 Åšr. S. (II. 6. 16-23) first sets out the views of GÄá¹‡agÄri, Taulvali and Gautama and then refutes them. GÄá¹‡agÄri held that out of the three paternal ancestors piá¹‡á¸as should be offered to those that were dead and those that were alive should be honoured in person, since the Å›rÄddha rite is meant for pleasing the ancestors; Taulvali opined that piá¹‡á¸as should be offered to all the three ancestors, whether living or dead, since in the rite of Å›rÄddha they are only a secondary matter (a detail). Gautama thought that piá¹‡á¸as should be offered to dead ancestors, upto three from the father if the latter was living, from the grandfather if he was alive and from the great-grandfather if all the three be living. Ä€Å›v. replies; one cannot offer piá¹‡á¸as to three ancestors 1110 beyond the father, grand-father or great grandfather, since one has no adhikÄra to do so; one cannot offer piá¹‡á¸as to an ancestor after whom there is an ancestor (within three degrees) living. One may offer homa into fire to those that are alive. (If all three ancestors are alive ) all the three piá¹‡á¸as must be cast into fire or he may not begin the rite at all. The KatyÄyana Å›r.416 sÅ«tra provides that piá¹‡á¸as are offered only to deceased ancestors; therefore if a person’s father be alive or if there is a deceased ancestor between whom and the performer there is an ancestor that is alive, then one whose father is alive can only perform homa (into fire, but no piá¹‡á¸adÄna) or he should not undertake at all (the rite of piá¹‡á¸a-pitá¹›yajÃ±a or pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha); that JÄtÄkará¹‡ya prescribes that no piá¹‡á¸adÄna is possible when a living ancestor (father) intervenes between the performer and a deceased ancestor, since a Å›ruti text says ‘one does not offer piá¹‡á¸as to ancestors that are beyond a living ancestor.’ Manu (III. 220-222 ) deals with this question as follows: If the performer’s father be alive he should offer piá¹‡á¸as to three ancestors beginning from the paternal grand-father or he may request the father to dinner as he would do to a stranger brÄhmaá¹‡a and offer piá¹‡á¸as to the deceased grand-father and great-grand-father, If the father be dead and the grandfather be alive, he may offer a piá¹‡á¸a only to the father and the great-grandfather (i.e. only two piá¹‡á¸as) or the grand-father who is alive may be invited to dinner as if he were the stranger brÄhmaá¹‡a invited to represent a deceased grand-father or if permitted by the grandfather who is alive he may offer piá¹‡á¸as to father, great-grandfather and great great-grandfather.

The Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra (chap. 75) has several rules of a similar character. Some of the PurÄá¹‡as such as Skanda VI 225.24-25, Agni 117,58-59 deal with this matter. Gobhilasmá¹›ti (II. 93 ff) has a long passage on this question, one verse of which is quoted below417. Many of the commentaries and digests hold divergent views on this subject, viz. the Mit. on YÄj. I. 254, Kalpataru (Å›r. pp. 240 ff), Åšr. K. K. pp. 552-556 and Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (III, pp. 499-503 ). It is impossible to reconcile all the views.The Kalpataru (on Å›r. p. 240) states that there are three alternative courses for him whose father is alive; (1) he should pass over the father that is alive and offer piá¹‡á¸as to those three ancestors to whom his father offers piá¹‡á¸as: (Manu. III, 220, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 75.1); (2) he should simply offer homa into fire as stated in AÅ›v. Sr. S. quoted above: (3) he should not engage in the rite of piá¹‡á¸apitá¹›yajÃ±a or pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha at all (Gobhila-smá¹›ti II. 93). The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu says that there are numerous alternatives mentioned by different writers, that they are forbidden in the Kali age and that one view is that a man whose father is alive cannot engage in a pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha and that the real conclusion is that Å›rÄddha may be offered to those to whom the father (of the performer) who is living offers piá¹‡á¸as.

About PÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha by jivatpitá¹›ka

It is clear that one whose father is living should have nothing to do with piá¹‡á¸adÄna for deceased ancestors. Manu (in latter half of III. 220) allowed an alternative viz. one should feed one’s father at a dinner (and honour him with the details of worship such as gandha, dhÅ«pa, dipa) and offer piá¹‡á¸as to the deceased grandfather and great-grandfather. When any one or two out of the three paternal ancestors are alive and Å›rÄddha is permitted to a descendant several alternatives become possible by permutation and combination, which are passed over here from considerations of space and utility. But the various views held by different writers as described above show clearly what a firm grip the theory of the supreme benefits to be derived from Å›rÄddhas had on the minds not only of common people but of learned men, who, in spite of the fact that the father was alive and was there to offer piá¹‡á¸as to his three ancestors, allowed even the son (whose father was living) to offer piá¹‡á¸as to the same three ancestors. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. extends the procedure about ‘jivatpitá¹›ka’ to one’s mother’s paternal ancestors ( changing the mantra where necessary )418. Similar rules apply where the performer’s mother is alive (AgnipurÄá¹‡a 117.60 ’evam mÄtrÄdikasyÄpi tathÄ mÄtÄmahÄdike’).

It is provided by Gobhilasmá¹›ti (III. 157) that a Å›ruti injunction should be made effective by means of anukalpa (of some substituted procedure) if the primary procedure cannot be carried out419. If a person cannot secure several brÄhmaá¹‡as but can secure only one, he should perform PÄrvaá¹‡a-Å›rÄddha in which six piá¹‡á¸as are to be offered with a single brÄhmaá¹‡a, provided he is pankti-pÄvana420 (sanctifier of a row of diners) and in such a case, naivedya should be offered in place of the dinner to the daiva brÄhmaá¹‡as and the food should be thrown into fire; so says Åšaá¹‡kha 14.10.

If even a single brÄhmaá¹‡a cannot be had for a pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha, then one should prepare with kuÅ›as the effigy of brÄhmaá¹‡a students (baá¹­u) and the performer should himself ask the questions and give the answers required in pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha421.

When one cannot secure a brÄhmaá¹‡a nor materials, or when one is on a journey, or on the birth of a son, or when one’s wife is in her monthly illness, one may perform ÄmaÅ›rÄddha (ÅšrÄddha with uncooked grains)422. This is stated by the SkandapurÄá¹‡a VII.1.206.52. Katyayana and SaurapurÄá¹‡a. 19.32 have similar verses “a twice-born person should perform ÄmaÅ›rÄddha when he is on a journey or is in distress, or if he has no fire for cooking and those who are weak may always perform it.” The MadanapÄrijÄta (p. 483) states that one who has the adhikÄra for pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha can alone offer ÄmaÅ›rÄddha. HÄrÄ«ta says that when there is an obstacle in performing a Å›rÄddha, ÄmaÅ›rÄddha is prescribed except in the case of mÄsika and sÄmvatsarika Å›rÄddhas423. Ä€maÅ›rÄddha is always prescribed for Å›Å«dras. It was further provided that the grains offered in Å›rÄddha should be utilized by the brÄhmaá¹‡as for eating the cooked food for themselves and were to be applied for no other purpose (HemÄdri, Åšr. p. 1527). VyÄsa provides424 that the quantity of grains should be at least two or three or four times as much as the grains required for offering cooked food. Some words employed in the various items such as ÄvÄhana425 svadhÄkÄra and dismissal of the brÄhmaá¹‡as had to be changed e.g. in ÄvÄhana the verse employed is ‘uÅ›antastvÄ’ (VÄj. S. 19.70) which ends with the words ‘haviá¹£e attave’ (which means ‘in order to eat the havis’) the words ‘haviá¹£e svikartave’ would have to be substituted.

Ä€maÅ›rÄddha and HemaÅ›rÄddha

Ä€maÅ›rÄddha is to be performed in the first part of the day, ekoddiá¹£á¹­a in the noon, the pÄrvaá¹‡aÅ›rÄddha in the afternoon and vrÌ¥ddhiÅ›rÄddha in the first part of the day (divided into five )426.

If even uncooked food grains cannot be offered the performer should perform hemaÅ›rÄddha (Å›rÄddha with money ). When the sun passes from one sign of the zodiac into another, in default of food and brÄhmaá¹‡as, on a journey, on the birth of a son, in an eclipse, in the case of women and Å›Å«dras hemaÅ›rÄddha is allowed or when one’s wife is in her monthly illness. In ÄmaÅ›rÄddha twice the quantity of grains required in offering cooked food and in hemaÅ›rÄddha four times (the price of the grains required for cooked food) has to be offered. If no money can be had the digests prescribe several modes which have already been set out above ( vide p. 425, n. 955).

  12. This idea is at least Indo-Iranian, if not Indo-European, The ancient Parsi scriptures speak of Fravashis that were originally the same as pitá¹›s of ancient Hindu works or the Manes among the ancient Romans. They were the everlasting and deified souls of the dead. Gradually the meaning of Fravashi was extended and even Gods and objects like the Earth and the sky were supposed to have each a Fravashi. Vide S. B. E. vol. 4 p. 262 for the Fravashis of the holy Yama and for the different classes of Fravashis; also S. B. E. vol. 23 pp. 180, 184, 230. ↩︎

  57. The Full Moon night of MÄgha is said to be the month of the year i. e. the year began on that day in ancient times. The Aá¹£á¹­akÄ day after the Full Moon day was the first and most important festival after the Full Moon and it was younger than the beginning of the year. It is probable that because of this it was spoken of as the wife of the year. ↩︎
